"If you ask me why I have crone upon
the stage, I can tell you in a word that
I found It necessary to have an object and
an ambition, and that' my tastes and in
clinations led me to Fek them on the
Etage. Had my tastes or inclination^
pointed elsewhere I should have followed
them there. I believe that a woman is en
titled to 6ek an object in life and she
ehould in seeking it consult her own abili
ties and knowledge of her own aptitudes.'
I consider my most probable chance of
success to be on the stage.
"I am aware thai to many persons this
may Â«eem a radical Btet>. There Is -a
prejudice of ancient standing against the
stage and particularly against the stage
woman, be her talents ever so great or her
success ever so gratifying. The same per
sons who will enjoy a theatrical perform
ance look upon the footlights as separating
them from a phase of life full of myster
ies and danger* and inhabited by a race of
beings whose status is different from thai
of the rest of mankind. "

Government Authorities in Mexico
Superintend Work Where the
Terrible Disaster Occurred.
AUSTIN, Feb. 4.â A dispatch from Sa
blnas, Mex., says the number of known
dead in the Hondo mines, near here, has
grown to 115 and It Is believed that there
are at' least twelve more dead bodies in
the underground chambers.
The task of removing the debris is stu
pendous. The workmen are conducting
the search for dead bodies under the di
rection of the Government authorities
To-day five miners were round alive in a
remote part of the mine. They were im
prisoned by falling coal. They had been
without food and water for more than
seventy hour* and were in a pitiable
condition. Most of the dead bodies have
been buried.
Burglar Has Disastrous Pall.
REDDING, Feb. 4.-Frank St. Claire
lies In the County Jail, unable to appear
to answer to a charge of burglary be
cause of injuries received while gaining
entrance to a local hardware store. St
Claire arranged to Fllde down a rope froni
a skylight, but fell from the ceiling to the
floor, striking on a stove. He managed to
loot the Â«tore. however, and carry. off his
plunder, but was later arrested and con
fessed. That was on Sunday. To-day he
could not arise from his prison bed and
was in great pain as the result of internal
injuries resulting from the fall. He may
not recover.

property owners ' of : Colfax are not
alarmed. over the preliminary survey now
being made by the Southern Pacific Com
pany. The-.survey for the big tunnel has
been completed. It will enter from the
east, near the level of Donner Lake and
emerge on the Yuba. north of Soda
Springs, a distance of five miles and 800
feet. After leaving the giant tunnel the
line was run for abdut three miles down
the Yuba, when, . owing to storms, the
surveyors' corps repaired to Newcastle
The company will run lines from the big
tunnel, irrespective of present towns, to
ascertain if a line can be found not to ex
ceed a 1 per cent grade or a 4 degree
curve. Should the company conclude- to
bore the great tunnel, it would take five
years work and until completed the cut
off line would not be built. Your corre
spondent has it from high authority that
there will be no abandonment of the main
line to a point east of Colfax, as It now
touches the leading towns and passes
through the* center of. the vast fruit
growing and fruit-shipping districts

William F. Cody, who 'afterward became
famous as "Buffalo Bill," rode In one con
tinuous trip 384 miles without stopping,
except for meals or to change horses.
President Lincoln's first message was car-'
ried from the Missouri River to Sacra
mento in seven days and seventeen hour
This is supposed to have been the quickest
trip for so great a distance that has ever
been made on horseback. With such rid
ing the regular time for letters between
San Francisco and New York was reduced
The quickest time ever made between
San Francisco and New York, by the over
land mail over wnat was known as the
Butterfield route was twenty-one days.
The pony express shortened this time to
ten days, according to the excellent nis
tory of Theodore H. Hittell. The railroad
superseded this service. Nearly 500 horses
were kept to do the pony express service.
There were 190 stations and 200 station
keepers and eighty-wonderful riders. Each
rider usually rode three horses, or about
seventy-five miles. .. . .
Here they were met by an enthusiastic
crowd with bands and torches. A proces
sion was formed and with music and con
tinuous cheers they were escorted to the
postoffice. ' " â¢â¢ -- , *
Pony Express Riders.
The opening- of the Central Pacific road
to traffic between the East and the Gold
en Gate marked the beginning of a new
period, vhlch had in it as picturesque
features as the p/rst had possessed; But
there had earlier been, exhibited in the
overland â¢ transit daring Â¦ and dash that
make the blood still tingle when the
story is told, for there comes Into -view,
rising and falling as-a fiery horse speeds
across the plains toward the Sierras on the
journey to California, the rider :of the
pony express. He is gaunt, bronzed with
weather to the color of copper,' keen-eyed,
fearless of Indians or wild beasts, so bound
to his steed by the habit -of riding- that
he might well be a centaur. The first
pony mail left Sacramento on the Pacific
side and St. Joseph, Mo.,; to cross the
mountains and plains on April 3, I860. The
mail from' St. Joseph coming west passed
from rider to rider by way of the South
Pass. Salt .Lake, the Humboldt- River and
Carson Valley i to Sacramento, which it
reached on. April 13. The news of its
coming was hailed with great enthusiasm.
The California Legislature was In session
at Sacramento, and both houses adjourned
in honor -of the event and to -give "rider"
and mall welcome. It came in just in
time for the regular afternoon steamboat,
and the horse, rider and mailbag, just as
â¢they had come to Sacramento, took pass
age on the. boat and arrived at the wharf
at San Francisco on the morning of
April 14.
Although the memory of Californians is
apt to linger with fondness upon the an
ticipations that preceded the actual com
pletion of the first transcontinental' railr
road and to dwell upon the thrill* that
came to them when they knew that the
old overland rail, across which so many
thousands had painfully passed in "prairie
schooners," was banded from end to end
with iron and that "the States" were
thereby- brought immeasurably nearer to
them, it is unnecessary to recount the
history of the subsequent doings of the
railroad builders at any great length. , '
There was a mad, frenzied race east
of the. Sierras to meet the Union Pacific,
Â¦which was building westward as fast as
possible. Then there came the junction.
The meeting of the trains, with the spec
tacular driving of a golden spike from
California into a tie -of California laurel
by Senator Stanford has been pictured.
Days of Romance.
Every town on; the-'Central Pacific line
in Placer. County has a history of great
picturesqueiiess. -;i. There human nature
escaped -.the" shackles of conventionality
and the buoyant spirits of the brightest
and- most daring, young men of the land
had free play in a theater which nature
had provided on a gigantic scale. As
early as June, 1S65, the road "had been
completed to Clipper Gap. In the follow
ing September the line was at Colfax. In
1S66 thirty miles of road over the' Sierras'
were completed. In 1867 forty-seven "miles
were added. -Snowsheds, telegraph lines
and puffing construction trains were now
a familiar sight among the Immemorial
lairs of the grizzly bear and the mountain
lion. The last mentioned year saw the
completion of the line to the border of
California, near Verdi. The line has re
mained in its original location over the
snowy heights of the tall : SIerras to . this
day, unchanged, a period of nearly thirty
five years. In that period a new genera
tion of the sturdiest of California moun
taineers has grown up in the Sierras, who
nave bought land, made their homes and
established themselves in business.
Each successive terminus that was
established served to be the distributing
center for, the mines,, wnich then were
the â¢ seen* of the Â¦ feverish activity of
thousandSÂ»of men. 'There -was an enor
mous teaming business : over Â¦ the moun
tains. During the 'period of construction
of the Central pacific, land" before, it was
operating, . a statement - was . made by a
hostile -voice, .which has never been con
troverted, thai in one year the receipts for
freighting over the' Sierras; to V'Vtrginia
City, via Placerville, brought in the great
sum of $13,000,000. This was-; exclusive of
return freights -and r passenger -business.
This statement was used to show that- the
Central Pacific, which" was then- denounced
as the "Dutch -Flat swindle" ty its
enemies, was really only a scheme v to run
a railroad. out to the mines and secure
the profits from freighting. to the miners.
Centers of Distribution.
buying necessities. As for the hordes cf
white men who .-were accompaniments to
the construction of the first line to bind
the East and, the West together, they
spent their money freely, with the accus
tcmed liberalty of- all early Californians.

In his weekly report to the War Office
Lord Kitchener states that for the week
ended February 1 twenty-nine Boers were
killed, six wounded, 142 taken, prisoners
and forty-eight surrendered.
The Premier has fixed the restoration of
peace in South Africa as the appropriate mo
ment to resign office. If the war is ended
Lord Salisbury will retire at the conclusion of
the present Parliamentary session, but it is his
present intention to retain his post until peace
is accomplished.
It is said the retirement of Lord Salis
bury from office will be coincident with
the end of the South African war. The
St. James Gazette to-day . claims it has
the highest authority for saying:
Salisbury May Retire.

Captain Cook, who commanded the

considered his brief, after he had learned
the views of Captain Clark, who com

President Considers Evidence.

LOS ANGELES, Feb. 4.â In an applica
tion for divorce to-day Mrs. Laura Ward,
formerly of Portland, told an unusual
story to Judge \ Allen. She said she was
forced to leave her husband, L. A Ward,

pelling her to live, as he did, on uncooked
fruits and nuts.

SAN JOSE. Feb. 4.â The meeting of the
general committee appointed to arrange
for the convention of the League of Re
publican Clubs here in 'April was en

ernment faction and the "gang" wing of
the party. Mackenzie's followers had
caucused and when Â¦ they came to the
meeting were prepared, to carry out a
programme.^
There are fourteen committees to be
appointed. -At first a motion that the
Good - Government faction be v given six
committees to\name. was adopted. Then
D. H. Bryant Introduced a, resolution,
which was adopted, giving the Mackenzie
faction the- following â¢- committees:
Finance/ mass-meeting, invitation, enter
tainment, music, hotels, transportation
and badges. This leaves to the opposition
the ' committees on auditing, souvenir,
press, hall, . publicity and promotion and
decorations. .

Mrs. Whittaker, Â¦ who conducts . the
Repellier lodging-house at .781 Sutter
street, last night Informed the local police
that Mrs. Hammond Moore, who was a
guest at her place, left for the East on
January 18. Mrs. Moore said that she
would stop at Sacramento to visit a
friend, who wasto accompany her to New
York. Mrs. Moore took with her three or
four large trunks, which were filled with
sealskins and the finest of clothing. She
was plentifully supplied with money
The theory is advanced by some of the
former acquaintances of Mrs. Moore that
the Stockton suicide may have been some
friend of- Mrs. Moore, who, knowing of
the latter's departure for the East as
sumed her name to hide her own identity

Two San Bernardino Judges Rule
That Certain Subdivision Is .
. Unconstitutional.
SAN BERNARDINO. Feb. 4.â Judges
O*-ter and Bledsoe of the Superior Court
to-day rendered a decision declaring sub
division 3 of section 647 of the Penal Code
unconstitutional. This section defines the
crime of vagrancy and subdivision 3 holds
that a person is guilty of being a va
trant when he roams about from place
to place without any lawful business.
This the court holds is in violation of the
constitution of the United States, as well
es of the State, as It tends to interfere
with the Inalienable rights vested in
every person. . .
The case at bar was the People vs.
"Jim" Goddard. He was sentenced by
Justice Root of Needles to spend ninety
days in the County Jail for vagrancy.
The complaint charged him with violat
ing subdivision 3 of section 647. His at
torney appealed the case to the Superior
Court. Arguments were heard by Judges
Oster and Bledsoe, sitting in bank, and
to-day the decision was rendered, both
Judges concurring In the opinion that the
Eubdlvision was unconstitutional.

On the day of the homicide Mueller had
an altercation with a eon of Mrs. Xutz
and attempted to strike him with an ax.
After this, according to the testimony of
the defendant, Mueller went to her
cabin. When 6be appeared at the door
and told him to go away, he drew a pistol
and shot at her. She ran in and got a rifle
and again told him to leave. He raised
his arm as though to fire again and she
ehot him dead.
At the time of the killing the Grand
Jury was In session. It investigated the
case and brought in an indictment against
the old lady. This was on December 9
and since that time ehe has been held
without bonds. The trouble that led up
to the tragedy had been of long stand
ing and resulted from a dispute over a
piece of Government property. Mrs. Nut*
forbade Mueller to come on her premises.
Ke paid no attention to this warning, but
insisted on crossing her land and other
wise annoying her.
The trial of the aged prisoner occupied
ten/ days and never was more Interest
displayed In a court case In San Benito
County. It was evident from the time of
her arrest that the sympathy of the com
munity was with Mrs. Nutz and there was
general rejoicing when the verdict giv
ing her freedom was announced. Fifty
witnesses were examined, the gTeater
number being from. Monterey County and
the lower end of this county, the scene
of the homicide.
HOL.LI&TER, Feb. 1â Mrs. Anne Nutz,
a' feeble woman of JO years, was to-night
acquitted by a jury in the Superior Court
of the murder of her half-brother, F. S.
Mueller, last November. When the ver
dict was announced the aged woman arose
and attempted to thank the Jury, but be
fore she could utter a word she fainted,
falling into the arms of her son. She was
carried frdtn the courtroom, broken by
the strain of her imprisonment and trial.
Epedal Dispatch to The Call.
The Trans-Alaska-. Company's expedi
tion, which made the six hundred mile
portage from the Yukon to Ilamnia Bay,
Cook Inlet, encountered only one storm.
Several rivers that It crossed were open,
though usuafly frozen at this season to
a depth of many feet. The entire sum.
mer on the Alaskan coast was mild and
rainy, with a temperature similar to that
of Washington and Oregon.
The masters believe that a tremendous
submarine disturbance occurred during
the fall of 1899, when the entire western
part of Alaska was shaken by earth
quakes. Their theory is that a great sub
marine mountain range j was thrown up
at that time underneath the Japan cut
rent, with the result that the current was
deflected toward the American shore. In
support of, this view they quote the fact
that the western coast of Alaska has been
more foggy during the . past two years
than ever before and that the climate has
been much milder. The change in tem
perature has been quite gradual, the max.
imum having been reached this winter,
â¢when mild, rainy weather has prevailed
throughout Western Alaska, from Nome
to Valdes. Â¦ C
TACOMA, Feb. 4.â Several sea captains
sailing regularly to Alaskan ports say
that beyond doubt the Japan current has
changed its course during the past year
or two and is now sweeping several hun
dred miles nearer the Alaska coast line
than formerly. Captain Wallace of the
steamship Cottage City and Captain John
O'Brien of the Dolphin . are quoted by
Skagway papers^ as holding this view.
Marine 'ifi'en who recently reached Skag
Â¦way from the Aleutian Islands and other
far northern ports express the same opin
ion. . v

FROM SEATTLE.
Attention was called yesterday to the
necessity for proper landmarks to indi
cate the narrow channel at the entrance
to Oakland harbor by . the grounding on
tho north side of the channel of the
steamship Centennial, which was being
towed to Oakland. The local Government
lighthouse . engineers are- now trying i to
yet from Washington an appropriation -for
Centennial Buns Aground.
Four big sailing vessels came over the
bar yesterday afternoon in a fleeu Two
of them, the British ship. Langdale and
the French bark: Jules Verne, were towed
in. The German ship C. H. Watgen and
the British ship Wavertree sailed to their
anchorages. The latter vessel. In charge
of Pilot Miller, sailed to an anchorage
well down the bay, and as she threaded
her way through the mazes of shipping
the pilot's nervy seamanship excited much
admiring comment. The Langdale left
London July 31 and came by way of Hon
olulu, which port she reached in 150 days.
There she discharged all but 800 tons of
her cargo. Captain Hunter is well known
in this port. He served his apprentice
ship under Captain McAllister, the local
6tÂ«vedore and a former master of the
Langdale. Captain Hunter Is accom
panied by his wife, who is a daughter of
Captain Kelly of the old Langdale, who
wag also well known here. Mrs. Hunter !s
one of the principal owners of the Lang
dale. The Langdale sighted tho Horn
October 7, but did not get to westward of
the cape for nearly three weeks later,
owing to the prevalence of heavy west
erly gales. The Langdale's cargo consists
chiefly of liquors and cement.

hama was made by the German ship

French colony and who is very proud of
his vessel's good showing.

Steinberg's Boiler Is Condemned.
When the boilers of the quarantine ten
der General Sternberg were blown down
the other day for their regular cleaning
the poor condition of the boiler tubes at
tracted the attention of the engineer A
survey was asked for, and as a result the
tubes were condemned. ,The Fulton and
Unio!i Iron Works have been asked to
submit ; blds for replacing them. It will
be thiee or four weeks before the Stern
berg goes into commission again, and
meanwhile the quarantine doctors are
using the gasoline launch C. A. McNelll
lor boarding purposes.
Sailing of Sterra Is Postponed.
The sailing of the steamship Sierra has.
been postponed from Thursday morning at
10 o'clock to Saturday at 7 p. m. on ac
count of the lateness of the British-Aus
tralian malls. .The last installment of the
mail was dispatched from Great Britain
by the" Cunard- steamer Saxonia on the
2oth ult..but did not- arrive in New York
untH yesterday afternoon.- It- is due to
arrive, here Â¦ next Saturday evening.

The annual meeting of the- San Fran
cisco -Flycasting Club was held last night

usual interest was shown in the pastime
and all indications point to a successful
season. ' .. . .. â¢ :. _ '_. .'.â
The contests of the club will be held at
Stow Lake, Golden Gate Park, commenc->
ing on the 15th inst. There.will be con
tests" twice a month, ten meetings of -two
days each being held. ; No contests are,
scheduled from June 7 to August 23. This
is Intended. to afford the members amplft
opportunity to prepare for the open-to
the-world tournament which is being af
ranged. ..." > .,"' !Â¦'
' The. present intention .is to, hold this
during the last week of July or the finst
week in August, subject to the wishes of
competitors from the East. The. tourna
ment is expected to be a pronounced suc
cess. The club has ample furids at its
disposal and many valuable prizes -have
been offered \}y men interested in the pas
j! e Homer Fritch, C. B: ' HbllyWocfd . anfl'_

the club. It is purposed to form an hon
orarV membership limited to ten. ' This
will. bo restricted to experienced angers

World's -Fair. The club will be: formed
by the Oregon Fish and Game Associa

clubs be formed in Los Angeles.and other
places where suitable water can b.e found.

Joseph Hasman, a merchant doing busi
ness at Elk Grove, Sacramento County,
was arrested yesterday by United States
Marshal Shine on an indictment found by.
the United States Grand Jury charging
him with defrauding the. Government by
sending in a false return of his business
as Postmaster of Elk Grove. It is alleged
that in October, 1900, Hasman reported
the canceled stamps, etc.,' as of the value
of $194 93, when, in truth, such face value
was only $176 05, a. discrepancy of $18 88.
Hasman gave bonds in $1000' before a Jus
tice of the Peace , at Elk Grove and was
released.

Whitney Horses Are Coughing. â¢
LONDON, Feb. 4.âTruth says that
Nasturtium, William C. Whitney's Derby
candidate, has been coughing for the past
ten days and that It is understood other
Heathhouse horses are slmllary affected.
"When once this sort of thing starts,"
says Truth, "there is no telling where it
will end, but it is hoped it will not run
through a.stable which now contains so
many high-class animals."
The Cuckoo has taken first prize for the
most beautifully decorated houseboat in
all the celebrations held at Belvedere. It
.was sold because the business of Its own
er demands all his attention. .
It la the intention of the new owners to
remove the ark to Fetaluma Creek, where
they can enjoy the excellent striped bass
fishing which its waters afford. With the
ark goes a swift gasoline launch and a
number of rowboats. The ark has six
roomy sleeping apartments and a large
dining or living room, . the sides of which
are fitted with Persian couches. , , It is
provided with a big kitchen, with a com
plete outfit of cooking utensils.- -Â¦-Â¦
. Negotiations were completed yesterday
whereby the ark passes into the posses
sion of a club of.. ten sportsmen.. It will
be ..known as the Quo Vadla Club, and is
made up of the following: Will S. Kittle,
John B. Coleman,. A. M. Cumming, F. T.
Hayes, A. B. Harrison, Henry Landsber
ger, George W. Emmons, W. F. Bogart,
E." Painter and F. H. Bushnell.
For seven years the popular owner of
the Cuckoo has dispensed the most lavish
hospitality on the boat, on which he has
livfed during the summer months over
near Greenbrae, on the Marin shore. All
thy prominent theatrical' people, clubmen
and others from the East have at one
time or another been ' entertained by the
owner. â¢;'â¢â¢â¢ - .-. Â¦Â¦..',.

Mrs. "W. TV. Stone, wife of Professor
Stone, principal of the James Lick Gram
mar School, passed away last evening at
6:30 at her apartments at the Van Nuys
Hotel, 1001 Bush street. JMrs. Stone died
of burns received on Tuesday afternoon,
January 28. '
She was unconscious at the last, and
Dr. C. F. Pawlicki Jr., who attended the
unfortunate woman, says she succumbed
to a- series of complications arising from
her terrible injuries.
Mrs. Stone was drying her hair over a
gas heater in her rooms when suddenly
her tresses took fire, the flames being
quickly transmitted to her clothing. She
threw herself upon the floor, causing the
curtairuJ and draperies of the room to
ignite. It was due to the .timely arrival
and presence of mind of J. S. Starr, pro
prietor of the hotel, who heard her
screams, that death did not immediately
ensue. He succeeded in extinguishing the
flames, but not until Mrs. Stone's head
and the upper/ portion of her body had
been terribly blistered and seared.
Though in the midst of great suffering,
Mrs. Stone managed to explain that she
was in the act of drying her hair over
the gas stove when it caught fire.
Mr. Stone and his daughter, Mrs. N. P.
Cole Jr., of 1716 Pacific avenue, were at
the bedside. Miss Jessie E. Stone of Los
Angeles, another daughter, is lying crit
ically ill at Los Angeles and was unable
to come to the city. It is feared that the
shock resulting from the sad news of her
mother'n death may prove too great for
her to survive. <
Mrs. W. W. Stone Passes
Away at the Van
Nuys Hotel.

Wins Two Gold Medals

The members of the_ Bohemias Club
have been privileged to see this week two
Important examples of the work of a
Californian artist, now in Paris, that are
on exhibition at the club. The pictures
are from the brush of Jules Pages and
both have been honored with medals by
the authorities of the Paris Salon..
The more ambitious effort. "La Toilette
du Modele," is a study of the nude, almost
life size, and representing a female model
dressing. The study shows the artist at
his test and is a powerful and interesting
effort. The canvas is perhaps too crowd
ed, the detail in some degree detracting:
from the central figure, and, like all of
Pages' work, the subject Is handled In
more literal /than . Imaginative fashion.
But as an example, of flesh painting It la
of the cleverest and strongest work that
has been seen here. The figure Is grac
lully posed, the woman, half -kneeling,
half-standing, facing a mirror, and tossing
up ner hair with easy gesture. The flesh
Is admirably modeled and the drawing is
delightfully sure and free throughout.
The figure compares most favorably with
the specimen of Pages' work now tn^the
Mark Hopkins Art Gallery that deals
with a somewhat similar subject.
The other picture Is also a figure sur>
Ject, .titled "La Gltanella." and represents
a Spanish dancer. . This also is a medal
picture, but the reason for the distinction
is not so Immediately apparent. It is a
pleasant color harmony, but the figure
lacks life and roundness and the present
ment of the subject Is not of the most pic
turesque. "La Gitanella" was painted by
Pages on a recent visit to Madrid. But it
is pleasant food for reflection that a Cali
fornian is winning such honorable place
in the great world of art as Pages is win
ning for himself In Paris.

A daring daylight robbery was commit
ted on Clay street, just west of Mont
gomery, yesterday afternoon, but the rob
ber was speedily captured by Policemen
Steve Bunner and P. Herllhy.
V. Petrovich, a visitor from the coun
try, who is stopping at the Garibaldi Ho
tel, 222 Broadway, had just turned into
Clay street from Montgomery when a
man thrust him into a doorway, garrotted
him and snatched his silver watch, break
ing the chain. It was all done so quickly
that Petrovich had not time to call for
help. .
Policemen Bunner and Herllhy had been
standing" on the corner of Clay and
Kearny streets and Bunner started to
walk toward Montgomery, when* Alfred
Kadich of 11 Fifth street came running
toward him and said, pointing to a man
walking hurriedly along the sidewalk,
"That man has robbeci another man."
Bunner, tbok hold of the man pointed out,
who claimed in broken English, Â»"I've
done nothing." Just then Petrovich came
running up with a piece of chain hanging
from a buttonhole in his vest and said he
had been robbed of his watch by the pris
oner. Bunner | searched the prisoner and
found the stolen watch and piece of chain
In his pocket. - .
At tne City Prison the thief gave the
name of Manuel Marcus and' his occupa
tion that of a sailor. He said his agn
was 18 years, but he looks to be fully 30
years old.

Men In every walk of life honored the
memory of the late Lawrence J. Dwyer,
Supervisor of the city and county, by at
tending his funeral yesterday. Officials
of the municipal government and ( rela
tives and' friends of deceased crowded St.
Peter's Church on Alabama street, near
Twenty-fourth, and devoutly heard the
solemn requiem high mass celebrated for
the repose of his soul. The ceremony was
of the most impressive character, and
tears were shed in sympathy for the
widow and children, for many hearts
were sad at the loss of a departed friend.
Rev. Father* Casey, pastor of the
parish, was celebrant. He intoned tho
offices of the mass with feeling. The bier
was placed in the center aisle Immediately
before the altar, and many floral tributes
from dear friends reposed on the lid of
the coffin.
Throughout the mass the choir rendered
music appropriate to the occasion. Miss
Annie I. Roney sang "Nearer, My God, to
Thee" with depth and feeling, moving tho
mourners to tears.
Re.v. Peter C. Torke preached the
eulogy of the dead. It was a tribute to a
life well spent. It was not an address oi
praise, but a eulogy befitting the charac
ter of the decease^. He told of Lawrence
J. Dwyer's life of honest purpose and of
his devotion as a husband and father.
. "What his work for this city was," the
speaker said, "you all know, and there
fore he needs no encomiums from my lips.
His deeds speak for him. All pay tribute
to his earnestness of character, and above
all to his honesty as a man. From his
youth he was more or less interested in.
public affairs, and that he has go le
through that ordeal, through all Us
temptations, without a slur on his char
acter proves that he was an honest man.
and this I say la his eulogy. For honesty
is greater than any . worldly emolument
ana more powerful than wealth."
In conclusion Father Ttorke told of
Dwyer's interest in church work and how
he helped to upbuild St. Peter's parish by
advice and deeds. v_; . ;
The vast congregation then filed out of
the sacred edifice- and stood with bowed
heads, forming a lane to. the hearse.
Through this path came the city officials,
led by Mayor Schmltz and Supervisor
James Booth. In the procession were Su
pervisors Alpers, Bent, Boxton, Branden
steln, Braunhart, Comte Jr., D'Ancona*
Eggers,- Lynch, McClellan, Wilson and
Wynn. Secretary Fay and other attaches
of the Board of Supervisors, The judi
ciary was represented by Judges Fritz
and Conlan. Â¦â¢ Among others who attended
were Captain Spillane of . thePolice De
partment, District Attorney Byington and
Auditor .Baehr.
' The Builders' Exchange sent a delega
tion, and White Eagle Tribe . No, 72, Im
proved Order of Red Men, was also pres
ent in resralia.
The widow was escorted to her carriage
by Rev, Father McQuaide, who is a cou
sin of deceased, '-

Curtis; John 'McCarthy, secretary of the
Board of Fire Commissioners; A. B. Ma
giilre,. former member of the Board of
Public Worka;. former Superintendent of
Streets Martin Fragler; John Behao. as*

Witnesses Contradict Each Other.
On cross-examination Oppenheimer ad
mitted that he had been interviewed by a
machinist a number of times since the
murder, but that he did not know the
man's name. He had been taken by this
man to see Buckley at the Jail and also
to the. office of Buckley's attorneys. The
cross-examination . was carried out on
lines to show that the witness could not
have seen the shots fired and that it was
impossible for him to have reached the
door of his house in the time that elapsed
between the first shot and the last one
"He was about the same size and build,"
replied the witness.
Samuel Oppenheimer, a cigarmaker, who
resided at the corner of Howard and
Twentieth streets on the night of the
murder, testified that he was on the sec
ond floor of his house when he saw a car
stop, but not at the regular place. Oppen
heimer claimed that he ran. down stairs
when he heard a shot fired and that he
saw a man firing at another who was ly
ing on the ground- He was positive that
the man who fired the shots wore a dark
hat and was taller and heavier than
Buckley. â¢ .
"How did the man who did the shooting
compare with the defendant, Buckley?"
asked Special Counsel Dunne.
Stewart also contradicted the evidence
of Miss Edmondson by stating that the
man who fired the- shots at Rice stood
within from three to five feet of his vic
tim, and that anther man stood close to
the elbow of the murderer.
On cross-examination Stewart testified
that he might be mistaken as to the color
of the hat worn by the man who fired the
shots, but his impression was that it was
a dark soft hat.
He Slight Be Mistaken.
The principal point sought by the de
fense in placing Stewart on the witness
stand was to get his statement that he
believed that the man who did the shoot
ing had a dark hat on. Buckley wore a
light hat when he was arrested.
Thomas H. Stewart, a mail carrier, tes
tified that Rice was lying on the ground
at Howard and Twentieth streets when
two men, ran up to him and one of them
fired four shots into his body. Both men
then ran off.
.Peter Bernard Flaherty, 14 years of age,
testified that he was on the car on which
Rice rode out Howard street on the night
of the murder. Flaherty stated that he
saw a man get off the front of the car
and he heard shots fired. He could not,
however, Identify the man who did the
shooting. i
On cross-examination Flaherty flatly
contradicted Miss Edmondson by stating
that the shots that killed Rice were fired
rapidly, and that he saw no man firing
from the curbstone in front of the bak
ery.
"It was fully five minutes," said the
witness, "from the time the first shot was
fired until the man with the pistol started
to run away."
In cross-examination Miss Edmondson
stated that the shots were fired very
slowly and that the man who did the
shooting stood on the curb in front of the
bakery and fired at Rice's body at a dis
tance of fifteen teet.
Took Five Minutes to Fire Shots.
- Miss Florence Edmondson , was the first
witness called for the defense. She testi
fied that she was in a bakery on the cor
ner of Howard and Twentieth streets on
the evening of October 11, when Kice was
shot. She ' heard shots fired while she
was standing at the counter of the store
and looked out of the door.
"I saw a man firing at another, man on
the ground," said the witness, "and then
he put the pistol in his pocket and ran
down Twentieth street to Shotwell. The
man who did the shooting was in front
of .the : bakery, but I did not see his face.
1 did not see Rice clubbed. The man who
did the shooting had a black suit and a
hard hat on. It was a dark hat. I saw
a man with a white hat on. He stood
some distance away f?om the man with
the pistol, but did nothing."

There have been frequent reports that Queen
Alexandra would adopt the lily of the valley as the
coronation flower, but the Gardeners' Magazine of
London says the honor will be given to the rose,
partly, because it has been the national flower of Eng
land for generations and partly because it is the
favorite of the Queen herself, who has one of the
finest rose gardens in the world and is very fond
of it.

Professor â¢ Hewett of London told the British
Chemical Society the other day that a process has
been discovered by which whisky can be distilled in
a drinkable and matured condition, and that'as a con
sequence the "finest possible whisky" could soon be
sold profitably at retail for two shillings a bottle.
This is surely going to be a hilarious century.
In the Cooper family there were ten
children. Five of them had six fingers
and five of them had not. The greatest
peculiarity is that every alternate child
in point of age, has the extra finger and
those who are not six-fingered are blessed
with an extra toe. and those who have six
toes have webs between their toes The
extra fingers and toes have well-developed
nails. Ex.ictly the same conditions nriÂ»
found in the Gasklll family. e
Mrs. Gaskillwas married to Zaccheus
Gasklll thirty-two years ago and has re
sided in Dresbach since then V
Mrs. Gasklll's maiden name was Olive
Cooper. She doesn't know where she was
born,' but the family is probably of New
York origin. She remembers only that
she was a wanderer with the Cooper fam
ily at an early age, and that the Cooper
family were basketmakers and venders
they led gypsy lives and crossed the con
tinent from New York to San Francisco
several times.
Unique in the history of freaks is the
six-fingered family of Dresbach, Minn.
The family now consists of Mrs. Gasklll
and ten children. The peculiarity belongs
to the mother's side. â¢
F.ngers and Toe> to Spare.

THE LETTER "M" ON COINSâ J. M.
O., City. The $20 and $3 gold pieces and
the Bland sliver dollar are the only United
States coins perpetuating the designer's
name. Underneath the lower line of the
medallion of the gold pieces Is "J. B. 'L."
(James B. Langacre). In nearly the same
position on the .Bland dollar is the letter
"M." It appears on the reverse upon the
fold of the ribbon, uniting the wreath,
and Is, the initial of the last name of the
designer, George J. Morgan.

OUT of the discussion which has followed in
Great Britain upon the announcement that
Mr. Carnegie. has determined to supply his
castle at Skibo with a library at a cost of $50,000
there has come a good deal of information concern
ing what a rich man's library should be according to
British standards.
The Westminster Gazette, which has developed the
subject by interviews with leading book dealers, re
ports that a considerable number of them discredit
the statement that the cost of the library is fixed at
the sum named. It is assumed by them that Mr.
Carnegie means to allow that sum for the acquisi
tion of books, but that it does not include the cost
of binding them. Furthermore, it appears that in the
judgment of the experts the binding is a matter of
much more importance and of much more cost than
the books themselves. ,
One expert said that the entire $50,000 could be
easily used up for binding alone, and even at. that
it would not furnish Mr. Carnegie with either a very
big library or a very handsome one. When asked
if a library could be choicely bound for $25,000, he
answered: "Undoubtedly, but its size would neces
sarily have to be limited." He cited a distinguished
lady of London society who has spent more than
$250,000 upon the binding of her library, and went
on to say: "To-day in the. auction marts of Europe
books realize enormous prices on account of their
binding. Volumes by no means rare or unique in
themselves if offered, for sale with a binding, of
Grolier or Ganevari will excite the fiercest compe
tition among wealthy collectors. A copy of La
Fontaine's 'Contes et Nouvelles en Vers,' bound by
Derome for Mme. de Pompadour, realized 17,000
francs. The purchaser paid at. least 16,000 francs' for
the binding." .
The same expert went on to say it is not infrequent

With a well supported merchant marine the people
of the United States would profit immensely by the
construction of the isthmian canal, and the commer
cial competitors of this country are well aware of it.
Thus it happens that the opposition to the canal and
to the shipping bill includes almost every foreign
influence that can make itself felt at Washington.
Foreign ship-owners, merchants, manufacturers,
bankers and ship agents in American ports are all
hostile to anything calculated to extend and to
strengthen our growing supremacy on the world's
markets. We need not find fault with them for
making their opposition felt in every way they can.
It is their right to take care of their own. Our duty
is to take care of our own.
The importance of this link to the chain of com
inercial expansion is too plain to be overlooked by
any one who gives it anything like due consideration.
Without an adequate merchant marine it will be
hardly worth while to establish and maintain a De
partment of Commerce, nor would the isthmian canal
avail us much if we had no ships to make use of it.
At present upward of 92 per cent of our exports and
imports are carried by subsidized foreign steamship
lines. We canndt compete with foreign ships which
are more cheaply constructed than our own by work
men at low wages and are manned and officered for
much smaller wages and salaries than are paid on
.\merican vessels.
In his argument to Congress on this subject Mr.
Seabury says the enactment of a well devised ship
ping bill will insure orders and work for American
ship-builders for years to come, will give employment
to thousands of wage-earners, will create an exten
sive industry for the investment of capital, will make
our commerce independent in time of peace, will
provide ample transports in time of war, will give to
our industries rapid communication with all the ports
of the world, and will assure to the American people
over $200,000,000 annually, which is now paid out to
foreign ship-owners for transporting our exports
and imports.
Of these three so-called links the first and last
are imperative. The establishment of a Department
of Commerce is by no means so necessary as the
other two, and yet it seems likely Congress will pro
vide for it much sooner than for either of the others.
Against legislation in favor" of the isthmian canal*
and for the promotion of the American merchant
marine strong opposition is arrayed. That opposi
tion has always been able in the past to defeat the
two measures either directly or by indirection. The
opposition to the merchant marine bill is especially
notable, for, notwithstanding the fact that the Repub
lican party in its national platforms has repeatedly
declared in favor of :i liberal shipping policy, nothing
has yet been accomplished to put such a policy into
operation.
GEORGE J. SEABURY of New York re
cently addressed to Congress an earnest plea
for the enactment of legislation which would
supply what he called "three missing links" in the
chain of our commercial prosperity. The first of
these is an American built and owned merchant
marine, the second the establishment of a Department
of Commerce and Industries, and the third the con
struction of an isthmian canal affording the shortest
route to the Orient.
THREE MISSING LINKS.
ANSWERS TO QUERIES.
MRS. A. M. TOWNE and Mrs. C.
E. Worden were "at home" yes
terday at 1101 'California street
and several hundred callers
thronged the beautiful drawing-rooms.
It was the last reception day of this fam
ily for the present, owing to the Ill-health
of Mrs. Towne, who Is about to leave for
Santa Barbara. There was something of
a crush during the afternoon, as all those
who enjoyed the delightful hospitality of
these hostesses at their recent tea were
among the number, as were also many
who were concerned about Mrs. Towne's
physical condition and wished to offer her
parting good wishes for a speedy recovery.
The decorations were not elaborate, as
the elegance of the rooms left little to be
desired. A number of vases of cut floTf
ers and foliage were in evidence and the
artistic touch of the hostess was appar-

Such are the facts, which Mr. Hines himself does
not deny. Some method must be devised for pro
tecting the industries of the country from such evils,
and no better plan is at present in sight than that of
strengthening the power of the commission and hold
ing it responsible for the enforcement of the law. . . " "
Unfair and illegal railway discrimination is v one
of the greatest evils that now afflict .the industries
and the commerce of the people. By means of such
violations of the law monopolies are built up, indus
tries are injured, and in some cases whole communi
ties are made to suffer by reason of unfair advan
tages given by the railways to competing points.
Finally, the commission directs attention to the
fact that even where the. courts have sustained the law
the railways continue to violate it, paying no v more
attention to the courts than to the commission. Thus
the report says: "We simply call attention to the
fact that the decisions of the United States Supreme
Court have produced no practical effect upon the
railway operations of the country." V
The assertion of Mr. Hines that the courts, have
not made a single decision which "has impaired the
commission's power to prevent rate cutting" is met
by the statement in the report: "While it was un
doubtedly the intent of the tenth section of the act
to impose a penalty upon the corporation itself, un
der its peculiar phraseology it has, however, been
judicially determined that -the corporation is not
liable. The agent alor.e.can be punished." In another
place it says: "The tenth section as construed by the
courts does not punish otherwise than by a possibly
nominal fine a departure from the published tariff
unless there is actual discrimination - between
shippers.". The report then goes on to say it is not
practical to prove such discrimination because, in
addition to" showing that rebates have been granted
in .violation of law, it would' also be necessary to
show that similar rebates were not granted to other
shippers at the same time on the same class of freight
and under similar conditions.
The response to those charges can be found in the
report recently submitted to Congress by the com
mission. Mr. Hines says the commission has the
right to call for books and documents of the roads,
but the report charges that the railway officials
"destroy vouchers and so manipulate bookkeeping
as to obliterate evidence of the transactions." In
another place, in referring to specific instances of the
violation of the law, the report says: "On some of
the lines the vouchers and other evidences of these
illegal rates were destroyed soon after .the transac
tions-were completed."
Mr. Hines says: "The paramount purpose of the
law was to secure publicity and the maintenance of
tariff rates. It is the express duty of the commission
to keep advised and enforce the law. It has the
widest possible . powers of investigation, including
the right to call for the production of all books,
documents and papers, and it can require the District
Attorneys of thfe United States to prosecttfe all vio
lators. These powers have been upheld by the
courts. The commission, however, has not only
failed to devote its time and energy to discovering
and prosecuting violations of the law, but it has by
its supincness* acquiesced in and encouraged them
and has done all in its power to break down the force
of the act and make it a dead letter by constantly pro
claiming that the courts have made its enforcement
impossible, whereas not a single decision has im
paired the commission's power to prevent rate cut
ting." ,
The claim of Mr. Hines that/his allegation is "in
disputable" is invalid on. its face, because it carries
with.it the admission that the commission disputes
it. There is raised an issue of fact between the con
tending parties, and the country will have to decide
between them. It is to be noted, however, in the
first place that there is no dispute as to the practice
of violating the law. Mr. Hines concedes that the
violation takes place, and his only defense- is that
railways are to be held guiltless because the com
mission does not do its duty. . '
WALKER H.; HINES, first vice president of
the Louisville and Nashville Railway Com
pany, has issued a reply to the request of the
Interstate Commerce Commission for a grant of
larger. powers to enforce the laws .enacted to 7 regulate
railway rates. Referring to the report 5 of the com
mission concerning the practice of the roads in
making rebates to favored parties and making other
unfair discriminations in rates *Mr. Hines says:
"The indisputable fact must be kept before the pub
lic that the prevalence of rate-cutting is an unanswer
able argument against giving the commission more
power, because it is due principally to the commis
sion's utter failure to perform its manifest duty."

Dr. Metchlikoff of the Pasteur Institute of Paris is
reported to have come to the conclusion that the
whitening of human hair is due to the action -of a
microbe, and he is now trying to find a means of
getting rid of the creature; so we may as well be
on the lookout for the coming of a new tonic for the
hair and.be ready to take chances whether, it will not
kill the hair as well as' the wicked microbe.

Finally, let it be decided as the fact that Congress
seems to drift because the people are drifting. Thd
people started out in the belief that taking the islands
meant the acquisition of more territory upon which
American labor and enterprise would be exploited,
a new land to be settled by our race where it would
perpetuate itself. The recoil from this unfounded
anticipation is taking place. The cry of the Ameri
can Chamber of Commerce at Manila for removal of
all restriction upon Chinese immigration, upon the
plea that only Chinese coolies can work in that
tropical climate, is the raising of the first curtain to
reveal a fact. When the people cease to drift Con
gress will find its compass and rudder.
While tactics are under discussion was it tactical
for the American Superintendent of the Philippine
schools to order all the schools to celebrate Rizal's
birthday and read his appeals to his people for na
tional independence?
One not accustomed to American ways would nat
urally suppose that this means permanent aimlessness
and opportunism in Congress, and that men go to
sleep on the question, waking up only to pay the cost
of an army in the islands, get their change back, and
slumber again. One thread runs i through all the dis
cord of sound and opinion. This is the constant accu
sation that reference to Philippine independence spurs
the islanders to resist our rule and our arms. Sena
tor Hoar's resolution for an investigation of condi
tions there has been stigmatized as encouragement of
disloyalty and resistance. But it appears that other
things said in Congress and in- the country have ex
actly the same effect charged to Senator Hoar's reso
lution. Whenever it is advised that we hold on to
the islands because of their potential wealth, and for
what we can make out of them, it is evident that the
most intelligent Filipinos share with the least intel
ligent an aversion to being treated as an orange, to
be squeezed for its juice. The proposition to hold
them in the colonial relation, import Chinese to pro
duce a commercial surplus, and use the land and the
people as a means of material advantage to us, as
their conquerors, is what rouses that spirit raised in
them by Dr. Rizal, the patriot and martyr. An Ameri
can must concede the kinship of that spirit with our
own aspirations and. their realization wrought out in
our scheme of self-government.
The people hunt the text of the debate in vain for
any statesmanlike enlightenment upon the subject.
The net impression gained is that leaders like Lodge
and Depew and many others on that side are fearful
of their first expressions and impressions, when they
declared themselves opposed to taking and holding
any island or extracontinental territory, and the
Southern members prefer to criticize methods rather
than policies, because of the expansion sentiment in
the South. No issue is made on the main question
at all, and policy drifts.
Reading it one is compelled to admit that it does
not raise the . statesmanship of the Senate in public
estimation. Tillman has flung at Lodge and
Spooner,' accusing them of supporting in. the islands
a concentration policy worse than Weyler's, and they
have retorted that Tillman's people down South burn
negroes at the stake, and he has countered by. citing
the cremation of negroes in Kansas and Colorado.
Senator Dubois has boiled with indignation because
army officers criticize the opinions of Senators and
citizens, and upon that subject attacked General
Wheaton, making the tactical mistake of describing
Wheaton as "a charity boy sent to West' Point by
some Congressman." In the next day's debate the
other side rose all along the line armed with the
army register and flung it into the teeth of Dubois,
for it proved that Wheaton was riot a West Pointer,
but had entered the army from civil life, as a private,
like Miles, Shafter and most of the general officers
of recent prominence. It did not occur 1 - to Dubois
to say anything, and' the discussion went off pn the
Manila censorship of the press, and a cross fire be
tween Hoar and Money about the number of troops
furnished by Massachusetts and the _ South in our
Revolutionary War! Then Tillman broke out in a
pustular way to present the figures relating to the
slaughter of Filipinos by Spain in three centuries
and by us in three years, and so on, the jangle jarred
day after day. '. /
THE appearance of the Philippine tariff bill in
'the Senate has been the signal for a prolonged
and acrimonious debate, which has not been ad
dressed to the provisions of, the bill at al^ but has
ranged, wildly and at will, all over the subject of our
relations to the islands and the islanders.

decorative' arts. The passion for reproducing old
designs, in my opinion, is not due to lack of inven
tion or individuality on the part of the artist, but
rather to the condition under which he works."

To you who are here it is unnecessary to re
count her victories in the sweeter lines of life.
In literature and In - art, nor her triumphs in
the practical professions of medicine, of law
It was different once. When women were
the slaves of men, their beasta of burden, their
toilers without" hope or faith or light, not much
could be. expected of them whom they served.
They were not fitted, lor the duties of life, they
could not assume its responsibilities. It would
have been a crime against; their dependent ex
istence, and a crime against society and social
evolution, against the future of the race, to
have given them participation in the affairs
of men, in the government of their people, in
the establishment of a civilization. They were
merely the Instrumentalities of man's progress,
a . lower order in the kingdom of animal life.
But as the race developed and woman by her
love and faith and- her immortal aspiration
gradually gained the . respect and the esteem
of the sterner sex, as the home became the cen
ter of attraction, and* the family the center of
social existence, as the schoolhouse arose 'and
education , became diffused . among men and
women, as science and 'arts expanded and-es
tablished and controlled the elemental forces
and made men's burdens lighter, woman was
accorded a little leisure and an opportunity for
obtaining mental equipment; and the progress
of civilization and of literature and of social
development is marked by the advancement of
the standing of woman In the view of man.
We glory in her advance, not alone because of
theoretical admiration, but because she has
raised the rest of mankind with her. ' â¢
Evolutions in Conditions.
I am sure that the State and the nation can
safely confide to its women the working out
of this problem. To those who are Intrusted
the care, nurture and education of its children,
to those whb ' have ever been called upon to
support and sustain its men, the country may
surely leave the eventual disposition of these
questions. - . â¢ .
There is In my mind no more reason why an
intelligent American citizen of education and
refinement, woman though she be, should not
have in the affairs of our civilization, govern
mental and commercial, as well as social, at
least an equal right with the ignorant and the
debased of the opposite sex. I am not. com
mitted absolutely to the doctrine of woman's
suffrage and her equal political rights, not be
cause of any fear that she will not prove equal
to the responsibility but because I have not
given to the question consideration sufficient
to enable me to ' determine definitely whether
or not> are true the only arguments on this
subject which have ever appealed to the consid
erate man of the I world, namely, the selfish
suggestions that the busy activities of political
life will detract from our home life its main
attractiveness- and beauty â will remove the
sentimental ism of our- existence â and, placing
man and woman on the same political basis in
the rivalry of life, will lower the spiritualism
of woman to the materialism of man rather
than elevate the tendencies and inclinations of
man to the present plane occupied by the gen
tler sex.. Of course, there are men whose con
stant contact with the world and with the
rougher edees of society have not dulled their
sentiment or deadened their spirits, and, of
course, there will always be women of similar
type and character, but the general question is
one of evolution and. of progress rather than of
immediate and spontaneous solution, and its de
velopment may well be left to such hands and
heaits and heads as are now before us.

standard may warrant. '

arrogatlon of right , and by custom . we have
been prone to consider the weaker sex.

Opportunity for "Work.
' We meet to-day for the first time as a thor
oughly organized body. When we met in Los
Angeles two years ago and It was my privilege
to extend the word of /welcome wo were for
the most part a company, of strangers. What
I prophesied then has come true. We have
looked straight into one another's faces, and
through the eyes down Into the mind and soul,
until reading there honesty of purpose, can
dor of difference and unity of ultimate aim,
we can and do call . each other friend. As of
old, when they went up to the temple to
pray bo we have come to call down upon our
selves the blessings of larger friendships and
the continued growth through breadth of out
look, of purpose, of association of work that
"loves neighbor as thyself," but no more.'
Students in social science tell us that Â¦ the
promotion of human sympathy is the moral
force most needed to-day. This is but another
term for reciprocity and that is the fundar
mental theory of federation. I hope the key
note of every session of this. meeting will be
"reciprocity," give and take, always in the
spirit of true helpfulness. And the thought of
reciprocity must not be limited to those who
are "of us."- We are here to consider new
methods and larger plans. And nothing should
claim our more serious consideration than the
needs of the women and children of this State
that we, as women in organization, can be help
ful to. Consciously or unconsciously your pro
gramme committee has invited you . to share
in a programme which trends to this one
thought. In' sleeking a subject or cause that
'shall be the unifying one for all the States
In general federation they, too, must come to
this subject of motherhood ' and childhood ' that
alway and alway is dear to the thought and
heart and life of woman.
Growth of Federation.
Kindly words of welcome, graciously given,
always fall most pleasantly on the ear of the
sojourner within strange gates, but if the spo
ken word was all it might prove to be the
source of later disappointment. But you have
spoken the word of promised welcome to the
eye before speaking it to the heart. In all
the details of arrangements that have met us
from the moment we crossed the bay or cams
in from the mountains and valleys to this city
of clubs and homes â to this comfortable, at
tractive hall, with Its carefully thought out
preparations, welcome has spoken to us on
every hand.
Surely you have carried out the spirit of
Shakespeare, â¢ and more, when ho wrote, "Sir,
you are very welcome to our house; it must ap
pear in other ways than words." And yet we
would not have the words omitted, for with
them conies the expression of soul that can
never be put into /"things."
"Welcome." ' Magic of humanity and divin
ity In the loving, gracious word.
"Welcome." It is a home-light streaming
at the wayside, a pathway of light from the
dark and lonesome highway to the cheery com
fort and loving companionship around the
hearthstone, and whether It be the gleam of
the candle in the cottage window shining out
clearer and fainter that the glory of the dis
tant star or the radiance of a thousand chan
daliers flooding the darkness from a hundred
palace windows, both proclaim with equal
warmth, "welcome." "A hundred thousand
welcomes aremo more than one."
"Welcome." The word is music even when
uttered by the harshest voice. It interprets
itself in all languages. How pleasantly then
does it echo itself in our hearts when spoken
by voices that ring true and clear as a silver
bell with sincerity and affection and framed
in a language made sweet to U3 forever by
our mother's voice. , -
"Welcome." What response can be made
to this countersign of hospitality by guest or
wayfarer save to hold out both our hands with
open palms â to â¢ meet and cling to the loving
hand-clasp that is sister to the word?

As soon as the doors were opened for
the afternoon session the floor, which
was reserved for delegates and club
members, rapidly filled. The gallery was
thrown open to visitors and every seat
was taken, a few gray-bearded men being
conspicuous among the spectators. A few
minutes before the opening hour Mrs.
Robert J. Burdette, the president, escort
ed Mayor Schmitz to the stage. The fol
lowing officers were grouped about the
Mayor and president:

eral hours and finally selected Mrs. Kate
Bulkley, president of the Ebell Society
of Oakland. Her nomination was agree
ably received by those members of the
federation who were apprised of the ac
tion of the committee. Mrs. Bulkley has
long been ' an active worker in women's
clubs and stands high in the councils of
the federation.

Mrs. Robert J. Burdette
Tells of Advancement
of Woman's Federation
needing special care, there i3 large opportunity
to choose where the work shall be. -^
Believing in . the sincerity of our purpose
your city, through you, the honorable' Mayor,
has extended its cordial greetings and I thank
you for them. We hope so" to imoress our
selves upon your community that all good men
shall court our helpfulness and strength in all
reforms that strive to lift higher moral stand
ards to protect the home; and- that bad men
will fear the power of our organized force
when through influence and public opinion we
shall say we must have pure homes, clean life,
clean politics, pure religion and the millennium
coming down the road to meet us.
Our gracious lady of the Golden Gate, In
the name of your 7000 sisters, I thank you
again and again for your cordial hospitality.
It is worthy of your great name and your
greater self and there can be no higher praise
than this. You do not count the sunlit waves
of the blue' Pacific that kiss your feet. No
more do you think of numbering the dimpled
smiles that never weary of making sunshine
for your guests, nor do they pause nor think
to measure the gifts that pour like flooding
rains from out your bounteous hands.
The open door waiting our entrance â the Â«n
tertalnment provided for our pleasure and
respite â the every requirement of our neces
sities met before the asking â the kindly, sin
cere, open-hearted hospitality, echoes and re
echoes your welcome to us. Surely we can say
the lines have fallen to us in pleasant places.
Must Help One Another.
As women of California we must find in each
other reflected the strength. Integrity and self
polse of our own mountains â the sweetness and
overshadowing charity of our own blue skies â
the life-purifying purpose of the salt waves of
our mighty Pacific, and our hearts already
whisper to us that brighter and sweeter than
all our rosiest anticipations will be our so
journ in the city of San , Francisco and the
crown of all Is that the love of women is
sweet to the hearts of women, and so holding
your hands as you greet us, our hearts breathe
the sons of Frank Stan ton:
"Earth green and the blue sky above you.
And rivers that ripple your name, .
And a soul that is singing: 'I love you'
And dust take the red- leaves of Fame!
For a touch that is tender and human
. Is more than all glories that gleam,
And the beautiful love of a woman
Sets life to the sons of a dream.
Let us dream! Let us dreamt Let us dream!"

Mayor Schmitz Gives the
Delegates a Warm Wel
come to San Francisco
FROM early morning till late at
night Golden Gate Hall was
thronged yesterday by delegates
and representatives of the various
women's clubs of California, the
occasion being the first convention of the
California Federation of Women's Clubs.
The ball was tastefully decorated In red
and white, with the Stars and Stripss
and the bear flag conspicuously displayed.
Flowers and paims and ferns were lib
erally used in the decorative scheme and
gave the place a homelike look.
In the morning the committee on cre
dentials was present to receive" the dele
gates who registered upon arrival. ! The
ladies composing- the committee were Mrs.
F. E. Prior, Mrs. R. Cermony, Mrs. E. fc.
Dodge, Mrs. W. P. Coleman and Mrs. I.
N. Chapman. As each accredited delegate
entered her name was placed on the regis
ter and then she was. provided with a
badge. A'arious colors were used to des
ignate the district from "which each one
came. The San Francisco club members
wear yellow, San IMegO pink, Los Angeles
purple. San Joaquin red, northern green
and Alameda dark blue.
Since the organization of the federation
t-wt> years ago in Los Angeles, where but
rwenry-six dubs participated. Its growth
has been remarkable, ninety clubs being
now associated with it. These clubs have
a. membership of 7000, and are represented
by delegates In proportion to their mem
bership. Delegates were . constantly ar
riving during the day, the following hav
ing registered up to the close of the after
noon session;

New York Stock Market.
NEW YORK. Feb. 4. â There was, an 'attempt
on the part of an element of professional opera
tors to awaken some speculative interest in the
market this morning, but the general disposi
tion was to acquiescence . in dullness: and neg
lect after yesterday's disappointed expectation
of a decision in the Northern. Securities .case.
It is the general opinion in the financial dis
trict that any pronounced financial activity
must await the delayed decision in this case,
or some striking ' new development In general
conditions. The professional element which at
tempted to contest this obvious tendency this
HiOrning was making very moderate progress
when the announcement came of engagements
of gold for export on Thursday to the amount
of $3,750,000. The market then immediately
flattened out, the greater part of the gains
being lust and was neglected and lethargic
for the rest of the day. The. day's small busi
ness was largely congested In Amalgamated
Copper. -Southern Pacific, Sugar and Metropoli
tan. The dealings otherwise, even in the great
transcontinental and Pacific railroads and in
United States Steel, were of quite insignificant
proportions. Amalgamated Copper was; weak
on the sharp break In the London copper mar
ket and on the, first, reaction for copper in the
local metal market' since the market turned
upward. The strength of Sugar was based on
the recent stock argument o* a probable ex
emption of Cuban sugar duties. The operations
in Southern Pacific 'were largely In the hands
of the speculative pool in the stock. Metro
politan Street Railway recovered sharply, from
yesterday's -depression on the", assurance from
W. C. Whitney that his coming retirement
from business would not involve the liquida
tion of his hol'l'iig-s. There ,werÂ» some wide
movements among' the less prominent stocks.
Lackawarna rose 3 points ' higher than yester
day's high price, but reatted 4 points. Nash
ville. Chattanooga and St. Louis advanced ioÂ£i
points on. light transactions and without-ex
planation. Minneapolis. St. Paul and Sault
Ste. Marie â¢ stocks rose 4 and' ii points respect
ively for the common and preferred, but saved
net gains. of. only 2V4 and 3%. . American Ex
press and United States Express made sharp
gains. The engagement of-gold for- export- had
only a slight hardening Influence on the money
market, but the doubt as to the . extent to
which the movement may reach caused some
uneasiness over the outlook. It Is believed
that last week's large loan expansion repre
sented the shifting emigrations due toforeien
creditors to the" local banks, and that the gold
Â°? 5VÂ», h P f/ m^ nt of o loans - The declaration
of a V_'Â« dividend on Standard Oil. the Ram
as for me first quarter of last year and the
year before, was regarded as of some sienifl
cauce. as It was feared thatuhe recent fall in
the price of oil would lead to a reduction of tl'c
dividend. The market closed dull and heavy
the tone being somewhat accentuated by the
report of the rejection of peace overture*, bv
the British Government. Â«*_ enure.,, by

GENERAL SUMMARY.
The weather has continued cold and frosty
in all pans of the State during the week, mod
erntir.g slightly toward the close. Rain has
la'.ien in all section*, and hail, sleet and snow
Etirms have occurred in porlicns of Southern
California.
Orain prospect* In the Sacramento Valley are

period, and in all the central and northern dis
tÂ»;3ts wheat and barley are in excellent condi
tion; growth has been slow Curing the cold
wtatfcer. but there is abundant moisture, and
with warm weather the growth will be rapid
ar.d healthy. In the southern districts the Te
wnt rains have materially Improved the con
dition of Grain, and in raj y places a nearly
average crop is predicted.
Piowir.g and seeding are progressing and in
soinc- of the southern counties farmers are in
cr.'aEing their grain acreage. Green feed has
been fclightly damaged toy frosts in eome sec
tic:Â»y. but is Etjll abundant except in portions
of the south, end stock are generally in good
ccr.diticn.
Deciducus fruit trees and vineyards are in
excellent condition and have been greatly bene
fit'd by the continued cool weather, which has
checked too early development.
TJnprotectol citrus fruits in portions of the
Saa Joaquin Valley and Southern California
rnve been injured by recent frosts, but as

less'will not bs heavy. Same damage was done

SACRAMENTO VALLEY
Cold weather continued during the wesk.
uith heavy frosts. Rain Saturday night was
beneficial to growing crops. Green feed lias
bctii somewhat damaged by frosts and grain
hes taad& slow growth, but the cold weather
is considered generally beneficial, especially to
deciduous fruit trees. Grain is in excellent
<>-nditiCT! and with warmer weather will make
repid growth. Prospects lor heavy crops of
grain and deciduous fruits were never b?ttÂ«r
at this date. Pasturage is plentiful and stock
are in good condition.
COAFT AND BAY SECTIONS. -
Cold weather has prevailed during the week.
with heavy frosts. Rain has fallen In all sec
tiens. treatly benefiting crain and pasturage.
Greta leed has been slightly damaged by the
frosts and grain has made slow growth, but
in ether respects the conditions have been gen
erally favorable. Farm work has progressed

completed. An -unusually Jaree - acreage of

Ojunty. Tree and vine pruning are progress
ing and in Santa Clara and Sonoma counties
the acreage in vineyards and orchards is being
materially increased. Almonds are in full
bloom in San Luis Obispo.
SAN JOAQUIX VALLET
The "weather lias been cold and frosty dur
ing .the week, retarding the growth of grain
end causing some damage to green feed and
unprotected citrus fruits, but benefiting decidu
ous fruit trees and vineyards. Rain at the

d-tion or pralr. and pasturage. It is reported

In the northern counties grain is in much' bet
ter condition and will probably yield an aver
sg* crop. Fasturage is scarce In some ' sec
tions *nd stock are not doing very well. Plow
ins and eeedir.g are progressing. .

visible" supply decreased .731,000 bushels. On
these influences "May started %c up at.'78%c

turned the market held very well considering

business was largely confined to Manitoba

Butter, Cheese and Eggs.
Receipts of Butter are larger, and while the
market is still quoted firm, there is lesn tend
ency to advance, and some dealers think that
the ' toD has been reached. Stocks on the
floors are light, however, and as supplies of
packed goods are practically exhausted the
retail demand for the fresh product is larger.
Cheese is steady an^ unchanged,
Arrivals of Eggs are slowly increasing again,
but as stocks are light and the demand is
good, - there Is no weakness apparent. At the
same time the tendency among the trads is to
sell, aa everybody looks for a decline at this
time of the year.

kegs of Butter, pounds of Eastern But
ter, .986 cases of Eggs, cases of Eastern
Eggs, 2000 pounds of California Cheese,
pounds of Oregon Cheese and pounds of

The till In the shoestore kept by M.
Goldman at 128 Ninth street was robbed
yesterday morning by a daring thief, who
secured $110 50 and made his escape. The
thief rifled the till while Goldman - was
next door. talking with a neighbor. Gold
man had Intended using the money . to
pay- the shoe bill. " â¢ * â¢ . .. â¢ Â¦â¢Â¦Â¦Â¦Â¦
Ernest May, a produce dealer,, was
robbed in much the same manner last
Saturday morning. May stepped into thÂ«
yard in the rear of- his store at 1W
Ninth street and when he returned . a' few
minutes later he discovered that $15 had
been abstracted from the cash register.
It is believed by the police that both rob
beries were committed by the same t*i%Â»r,
Lose Their Coin While Away
From Their Stores.
CLEVER SNEAK THIET -
RIFLES CASH ! DRAWERS
Shoe Dealer and . Produce Merchant
LOS ANGELES, Feb. 4.â Al Neill. and
Mike Donovan fought twenty, rounds to
night to a draw. Neill did not perform
as well as he did In his previous matches,
being slower and not in perfect condition.
Donovan was in fine shape and made a
fast and determined fight. Neill jabbed
Donovan a thousand times with his left,
closing his ' left eye tight, but Mike was
there all the time and ready ' for more.
After the .sixteenth round , Neill tired
somewhat. and lost .some of his. .speed.
Donovan forced him about the ring for a
time and evened up matters enough to get
a draw. The attendance was 4000. .
Neilf and Donovan Fight a Draw.
The Board of Health sent a communica
tion to the Board of Education yesterday
recommending that the auxiliary rooms
Of the Hearst Grammar School, on the
corner of Steiner and Herman streets, be
closed for a period of thirty days. Health
Officer O'Brien says that notwithstanding
the repeated fumigation of the rooms
sickness has increased among the pupils.

Boxer Dies' of "Athletes' Heart."
VICTORIA, B. C.. -Feb. '^.-Yokohama
papers report a fatality in connection with
Boxer, Jack Slavln's visit to that city.
The ex-champlon gave a boxing exhibition
in which one of his opponents was a
colored man named E. F. r Pad more. So
unequally matched -were the pair â¢ that
Slavln told ' Pa"dmore to "hi.t harder" and
on his part did nothing more than â¢ deal
what appeared to the spectators to be
playful taps. At the end of the bout Pad
more showed no signs of punishment, but
toward midnight he became weak, com
plaining of chills and- numbness and a
pain in the region of the heart. Dr. Tay
lor of the United States Naval Hospital
and two other doctors were called In but
in Â«plte of their efforts he grew Worse and
died at 1:30 a. m. A post morten resulted
in the cause of death being declared what
is popularly known as "athletes' heart."

President Gompers, speaking* in behalf

nesÂ© exclusion was desired solely by the
Pacific Coast States.. He said It was
asked for by. all the organized wage^earn
ers, regardless of the section from which
they hail. . . .:Â¦
After fifteen years of careful study, he
said, it was his firm conviction that the
United States lost' her seamen because of
the treatment she gave them and he con
cluded by saying it was not- safe to put
the merchant marine of the Pacific Into
the hands of the Chinese. â¢
Asked to name the warships he' had re
ferred to, he. could recall but five and
mentioned the. Oregon, Iowa, Texas, New

tho. Washington navy-yard and out of
twenty-one men Â¦ aboard her only one was
a native American. â¢

Authority is given to the Interstate
Commerce Commission to hear complaints
against the fairness of such pooling ar
rangements and to make an order annul
ling the contract in the respects found ro
be unjust and unlawful. Failure to com
ply with the order will work the annul
ment of the contract. Proceedings by
writ of injunction are authorized against
any party disobeying the provisions of
the bill, and the offending party also la
made subject to a penalty oÂ£ $10,000, to be
paid Into the treasury of th.e. United
States. - Wherfr railroads pass into a for
eign country authority Is given in addi
tion to the imposition of fines to suspend
the movement of trains within the United
States until the requirement shall, b*
complied with. â¢ " ...
Authority Is given to the commission
to require the publication of traffic rates,
and where there is reasonable ground for
belief that freights or passengers are be
ing carried for less than the published
rates the commission Is authorized to be-
Kin proceedings in tho Circuit Court of
the United States, which court is re-,
quired to enforce an observance of the
published tariffs. These proceedings are
to be directed not only against the car
rier, but also . against the . other parties
Interested.
The section of the bill concerning pool
ing: provides that "It shall be lawful far
any two or more common carriers to ar
range between and among themselves for
the establishment or maintenance of
rates. It also shall be lawful for such
carriers to agree by contract in writing
filed: with the Interstate Commerce Com
mission upon the division of their traffic
or earnings or both."
The provision with reference to the fix
ing- of rates authorizes the Interstate
Commerce Commission to hear complaints
of discrimination made against common
carriers, and directs that any definite or
der made by the commission after such
hearing declaring- a rate regulation or
practice to be just and reasonable* shall
become operative and be observed by the
party or parties against whom it is made
within thirty days after notice. Appea!
to the United States Circuit Courts and
to the Supreme Court of the United
States is authorized. ThÂ© latter court is
given authority to suspend or revoke a
rate order maae by tne commission upon
application by parties affected, and all
such orders are limited to one yfiax'n
duration.
