This led him to speak of the triats of
the Filipinos, one of which he said is cru
elty. He had been told by the American
army officers of many instances of muti
lation of the corpses of. our soldiers. This
being true, it was not surprising that
there had been some retaliation on the
part of some- of the small bands of sol
diers. He had heard of no charge of mu
tilation, but there were probably some
cases of unnecessary killing, some cases
of whipping and some cases of what is
called the "water cure," a method of pun
ishment consisting of pouring water down
the throats of men until they would; swell
up, and- becoming frightened would tell
what they might know, aSt forinstohce,
to wher.t! guns could . be secured. In
this connection he called attention to the
orders of General MacArthur as to these
practices, which were, he said, very strict.
When these infractions of propriety had
occurred he was sure'they had been in the
face of direct orders to the contrary.
"I venture to say that there is not a
Filipino general or commanding officer
who could not be convicted of responsible
ity for the killing of their people." .
Governor Taft also said that the great
majority of the people of the islands de
sire peace, and that the insurrectionists
we're by their acts preventing the mass
of the people from settling down and
earning a quiet living. Instead of being
allowed to do this they are kept under a
system of terrorism, which should be
stopped.
Tho condition of activity continued un
til the last Presidential election in the
United States, said the Governor. Th6 in
surgents believed that if Bryan should be
elected there would be a change of pol
icy and the islands surrendered, . placing
those who were in insurrection in charge,
but when McKinley was elected there was
a feeling that there would be an indefinite
retention of the archipelago and that the
system then in force would be continued.
Discussing the effect of the guerrilla
warfare Governor Taft said it often re
sulted In the murder of , the Filipinos
themselves. ' â¢ ' . r
"The war has been all over in these isl
ands, and that which remains is a crime
against civilization. It is also a^ crime
against the Filipino people to keep up a
st ate of war under the circumstances.
They have worn out the right to -any
treatment but that which is severe and
within the laws of war."
Aguinaldo >aid that if he did not kill
Luna, Luna would kill him. Indeed, the
custom was to order a man's death in his
absence and have him killed upon his re
turn. The presence of these I guerrilla
bands rendered tt' necessary to placo gar
risons everywhere, there being at one
time as many as 500 of them. This ren
dered the work .before the army very
arduous, and the activity, the skill and
the bravery .of the American troops were
such as to soon render the enemy tired oÂ£
warfare. Speaking of the military policy
in the Philippines Governor Taft said:
Then, in order to give the army its just
due, he took up and analyzed its work in
the islands. He recalled that as soon as
General Otis had received reinforcements
sufficient for his purposes the Philippine
army had broken up and scattered into
gue.rrllla bands. On this point Agtiinaldo
and Luna had differed,. Luna advocating
the continuance, of the war on the old
lines. So sharp was this difference, he
said, that Aguinaldo, had directed and se
cured : the assassination* of. Luna, thus
leaving- the way open for the carrying out
of his own plans. He would not, he said,
report the incident if it had not been posi
tively reported to him by General I'uns-.
ton.

twisted iron, brick and wooden columns
enveloping them. -

We have the finest forestry specimens In

us and place them In the exhibition. One speci
men will be a sugarpine twelve feet in diame
ter. We would have liked to have taken speci

There is onlv one more remark I would like
to make: The California Legislature does not
mept until January" of next year and it will
then be too late to make an appropriation.
What we would like now is for counties to
make appropriations to boom their districts.
Should they spend money in this way we will
have all the more left to spend In other chan
nels. Several counties have already communi
cated with me and have appropriated hand
some sums' toward the exhibition. I am also
very anxious to get horticulturists and others
to make donations.

MoSes A. Hearne was 69 years of age.
He b/td followed mining for many years
and . success. He died owning prop
erty in Delamar, Nevada City and . other
places, and with $40,000 cash in a local
bank. The heir to all this property is his
only iiving relative, a son, John F. Hearne
of Redding and Dunsmuir. Twelve or
flfteen years ago Hearne went to Shasta
County and located the Hearne group of
copper claims near Kennett.. The Shasta
King is the principal mine of the group.
The enlire group was sold to the Trinity
Copper Company about a year, ago for
$30,000.
Hearne was a living thermometer.. He
lived for years at Nevada City and
worked as an amalgamator and assayer
until his system absorbed so much mer
cury that his final years' brought frequent
pain. Pie was keenly, sensitive to changts
m the weather and in winter suffered
acutely with .rheumatism of his joints.
In warm weather he raised erect to his
full six feet 2 inches. On a cold day he
could -hardly move about and his whole
nature was at the lowest ebb. He de
clared that he absolutely reflected the
size and dropping of the mercury in' his
system.
Moses A. Hearne, one of the best known
mining, men in this State, died early yes
terday morning at the Golden West Ho
tel. He had been ailing for several
months, and his death was not un
expected. â¢ â¢

As a member of the football team Ford
was accorded the privileges of the club
and was entered on the books as an
"athletic member." As soon as the board
of directors learned Ford or Davis' true
character they expelled him for "non-pay
ment of dues." Ford has cards for other
clubs, but will not be able to use the
magic pasteboards to gain admission to
tlio exclusive organizations. He created
a wido swath in local society on nothing.
Wh*rievÂ£r he desired to dine out or at
tend a cotillon' hp had 10 hire a dress suit.-
The following day he would dispel his
dream" of happiness by returning the
clothes. â¢ Thosp gentlemen who stood
sponsor for Ford will have a nice time
explaining their conduct.
Ford's method of operating in Indian
apolis was to secure an introduction to
some prominent society women, usually
married, and after securing a ring or
jewel from her pawn it. His dupes never
dared call him to account for fear he
would compromise them. He was arrested
at one time and but for the aid extended
him by a rich relative would have suf
fered long imprisonment. His picture was
taken at that time and duplicates were
sent over the country. A set adorns the
archives of the local police department. I
On December 19 he "registered at the
exclusive Knickerbocker. Hotel, on Van
Ness avenue, giving as references the
names of several highly connected people
of' the city. During his residence at the
Knickerbocker he was enabled to run up
a bill-wltb. Kelly Brothers, the liverymen.
It was in consequence of his indebted
ness to them that trouble ensued and he
was told by Mrs. Robinson that his room
was required. Ford left a two weeks' bill
behind him.
On November 10 Ford registered at the
Eugene House, on O'Farrell street, in
forming the proprietor that he had been
engaged to appear at the Orpheum. On
the strength of that assertion he was al
lowed to run up a bill of $30.
At 'the Palace Ford posed as a Ndw
York business man. He made himself
affable to all with- whom he came in con
tact and it was not until he was accomo
dated with a loan of JSO by a young
Southerner, a tourist from Tennessee,
that the slightest suspicion fell upon him.
He failed to return the sum and left the
hostelry.
The expose of this "social highwayman"
has created a sensation in social and club
circles. Although Ford has been here but
three months ne has by suave address
and abaundant nerve lormed acquaint
ances among the leading set of this city.
Ford made his first appearance here on
November 7 last. He arrived in the com
pany of D. Beeman of chewing gum lame
and took up quarters at the Palace. He
posed as a graduate of Cornell and gave
out the information that he had played
end 'on his college team. The Olympic
Club was looking- for strong material tor
its eleven and Ford was quickly engaged.
He proved a good player and was picked
to so to Hawaii with the team, but un
fortunately for him the trip fell through.
Society has received an awful shock to
its refined sensibilties. Discussion is rife
at teas over the fact that Paul Davis
Ford, alias H. F. Davis, alias Frohman,
alias Jules Harvey Ford, and many other
aliases, who is well known to the polico
of the large Eastern cities, had been re
ceived and dined by the first families of
this city. Society discovered too late that
the suave young Easterner, who ingra
tiated himself into the good ' graces of
matron and belle, and partook of hospi
tality willingly extended, had been In
prison for various offenses and that his
picture reposed in the rogues' gallery of
the Indianapolis police.

"I spent three weeks with a friend in
Dayton last winter. She was using
Postum Coffee exclusively as a beverage
at meals. I said, 'I thought you were so
fond of coffee that you could not be in-
duced to give it up?' 'Well.' my friend
said, 'we got to liking this Postum Cof-
fee, for it serves as a food as well as a
beverage, and we have all felt so much
better since leaving off the old coffee.'
"I learned to make the Postum and
made it fine, so when I went home I be-
gan to serve It to my husband instead of
the old-fashioned coffee. \ About a week
afterward I asked him how .be liked the
coffee, and told him I had been trying a
new kind. He says. T think it Is most
excellent, .is it Mocha or Java or a mix-
ture?' 1 told JiJm that It was the famous
Postum we had read bo much about and
he most jumped from his chair in sur-
prise, but the charge has become a fix-
ture and he will never give up Postum
and go back to the old-fashioned coffee,
for we not only like it better, but we feel
Â¦o much better in every way." This
lady's name given by Postum Co., Battle
Creek. Mich. ,THE S&N FRANCISCO CALL. WEDNESDAY, FEBRUARY 5, 1902.

A REPORT OF PROGRESS;.
The following extract from report of work
done to January 22 Is of Interest to share-
holders and others. The full report' will be
mailed on request:
"TACOMA. Wash., Jan. 27.
"Owing to the lateness of the season, we
established camp on the eastern side of our
property and built a mile of trail. We have
a comfortable camp â bunkhouses, cookhouse,
toolhouse, and will soon have a stable with
accommodations for 15 horses. â¢ â¢ â¢ We
have driven one entry 44 feet through hard
rock, but are now into a softer rock, which
Is a sort of gangue between the country
rock and the vein. This entry Is being
driven to reach a vein of ore which show3
over 100 feet on the surface, and gave an
assay of $12 SO per ton, principally gold.
This vein can be traced for 20CO feet through
the 'Stray Beauty Extension', and 'Lourette'
claims. We confidently expect to reach this
vein ten feet from the end of our present
workings. We have commenced a new entry
100 feet lower on the vein above mentioned,
and are In 15 feet We expect to reach the
vein about 40 feet from the beginning of the
" tunnel. There Is also a large vein of goid-
\ bearing quartz on the 'Stray Beauty" which
we shall endeavor to open as soon as cir-
cumstances will permit. This vein is well
defined, is 60 feet wide, and runs parallel
' to the one on which we are now working.
There. Is also a large vein visible on the
'Ben G'. claim, another on the 'Sulphide.'
and a five-foot vein on the 'Ruby Jewel.'
All these veins can be worked from our
present camp. The veins referred : to arÂ«
gold-bearing quartz. On the 'Mlnne Bay,'
'Lawton' and 'Mountain Chief cU.ims are
several large veins of copper ore. None of
these latter claims can be opened now ow-
-Â» Ing to the snow.
"It Is the Intention of the Syndicate to In-
stall a power plant for drills as econ as a
â¢ sufficient quantity of rich ore hs.s been
opened to admit of shipping. â¢ â¢ â¢ it u
' only a question of energetic and systematic
development work when our properties will
show great mineral wealth.
< "R. E. McFARLAND, President."

WANTEDâ
MACHINISTS AND HANDY MEN TO KNOW
THAT THE MACHINISTS' STRIKE IS
PRACTICALLY OVER. . *
ALL SHOPS RUNNING AS USUAL.
The machinists' strike that has been on In the
principal Denver shops since October is now
practically broken, as the shops are running
with non-union men and only about 25 per cent
of the strikers remain in town, walking the
streets. Â¦, .
The victory claimed by the strikers at the
Colorado Iron Work3 Company is In reality a
defeat. The men went out for a nine-hour day,
whereas this is the very point not conceded by
the Colorado Iron Works Company. A number
of the old men from the Colorado Iron Works
Company were' selected by the management and
offered individually their old positions, if they
would work the hours per day stipulated by the
manufacturers, and conform to their shop
rules . and regulations.
Mulberry, who is conducting the Ftrike, op-
posed this and a stormy meeting followed, un-
til the men demanded that they be allowed to
return or withdraw from the union. To make
the appearance of a victory and prevent a loss
of more men, the union were obliged to sanc-
tion the return of these men. hence the mis-
leading report in the daily papers.
This strike has demonstrated two things:
That the majority of flrst-class men are free
men and will not be bound by arbitrary unions,
and that the Denver Machinery Manufacturers
are organized as a unit to resist the unjust,
unreasonable, unbusinesslike and arbitrary de-
mands of organized labor.

We Â¦ fear that the Rooster will not have a
chance . to spread its wings over any further
political honors for the present Governor of
this State. Ex-Senator Bulla, from the Gov
ernor's own county, states emphatically that
Gage cannot control the delegation from . hla
home, county, and . from all appearances the
Senator is right. \ Â¦Â¦ ,
- The newspapers throughout the State
are taking an active Interest in public af
fairs. The Acton Rooster and Modesto
News do. not agree as to the future public
career of Governor Gage. The Rooster
having been first to name Gage for Gov
ernor now nominates him for Vice Presi
dent upon the ticket headed by President
Roosevelt. The Modesto News^ comment
ing on the Rooster's crowing enthusiasm,
says: . . : .... .
The convention could establish an order
of business providing that Judicial nom
inations should come first. The-Demo
cratic State Convention which assembled
at the Baldwin Theater in 1896 put Judicial
nominations first and James H. ' Budd
made it his special business to see that
Judge Murphy - of Del Norte was nom
inated for Justice of the Supreme Court
Later on-, when the Democrats talked of
nominating Barney Murphy of, San Jose
for Governor,, the embarrassment of hav
ing too many Murphys on the ticket be
came painfully obvious and to relieve the
situation of- its ' awkwardness Budd him
self accepted, the nomination for Gover
nor. . .----
. It is regarded as Â¦ certain that â¢ there
will be two candidates before the Re
publican State Convention for nomination
for the office of Chief Justice of the Su
preme Court. The incumbent, W. H.
Beatty, has many supporters throughout
the State, who do not hesitate to ayow
in favor of .his nomination. C. "H.-Ga
roiitte of Yolo, an associate Justice, has
many friends who favor his' promotion
to the chief place on the bench. The
Democrats are watching the contest with
unusual interest, and standing ready to
take advantage of any mistake that the
Republicans may make. Wise politicians
suggest that leading Republicans should
adopt measures to keep the contest for
the judicial positions separate from the
gubernatorial fight.
